Special note should also be made of how well the animators of this film were able to capture the desolate, broken world where the now ruined forest once stood.<br /><br />But the ultimate charm of this film is that, unlike the original story, a more detailed depiction of how The Once-ler and the absolute absurdity of consumer fancies is presented in a manner that is not necessarily anti-progress. One can't help but note how the environmental catastrophe that resulted from short-term binge harvesting could have been avoided with a gradual harvesting approach rather than just slashing and polluting the entire capitol source. Likewise, the whim-based nature of the Thneed product and how it caught on is reminiscent of how many fads begin, are milked relentlessly, and then discarded like the evening trash for someone else to clean up.<br /><br />This is among the better computer animated flicks I've seen in a few years, and it's a good all around fun fest with a reasonably nuanced message that is common sense for anyone who desires a quality world rather than a quantity based world of ephemeral highs and hangovers. 031603441X|2.0|My general aversion to memoirs would have prevented me from reading Richards' but my husband gave it to me last Christmas. I found the first 200 pages quite engaging. Richards' earliest memories of the post-war England of his childhood are of bomb craters and ration books, features of wartime deprivation that remained until the mid-50s. He describes a rambunctious childhood -- lots of ramming around on bicycles -- and typical early education -- a bully who is eventually bested and a 3R regimen that ignores huge swaths of human potential. His brief love of school coincided with his membership in a choir that took a lot of prizes, one he was booted from when his voice broke. He credits one teacher for steering him into art school, where he took up the guitar, the first step toward legend as the composer of the Rolling Stones' most amazing riffs. And it's the early days of the Stones which held the most interest for me. How from the first their marketing was carefully counter to the goody-goody image of the Beatles. How the Stones and the Beatles scheduled their releases so their new singles wouldn't hit at the same time. How their earliest gigs were revue shows which had them on stage three times a day for 10 or 15 minutes in three different towns. How they debuted in a music scene dominated by croon-tunes from the Everly Brothers and Beach Boys as the rawest part of a teen tide that simply swept those crooners away. Richards' recapitulation of the history of that amazing teen tide, which swept the entire western world, is definitely worth reading, worthy of three stars. The rest -- not so much. Richards' descent into heroin addiction turned him into a truly pathetic parent and the junkie story of getting hooking, finding the smack, eluding convictions, etc., offers no new insights into that misfortune. In fact, Richards never explains why he became a junkie and evades even asking himself the question. Why is a question he evades most of the time. Why his penchant for partners who are arm candy, even while he snickers at other rock-star cliches? Why he fell in love with the two women who bore his children? The last 350 pages are pretty tedious -- junkie tales, detailed explanations of chords in some of the later Stones' songs, gossip about Jagger and dish on their disagreements, yadda, yadda, yadda -- and typical of celebrity memoirs. That bit earns -- barely -- a one-star from me.partners who are arm candy, even while he snickers at other rock-star cliches? Why he fell in love with the two women who bore his children? B004EJUMT2|5.0|Ghost's songs to me sound like satan-worshipping gospel intertwined with melodic and thrashy metal. Quite unique and unorthodox, yet it works on so many levels. Some things to note about this album:<br /><br />-The cohesion of all the sounds. You could tell a lot was put into it when everything seems to mesh well, especially the blend of bass, guitars, drums, and organ. One instrument does not outshine the other, they are in complete harmony and balance with one another. This harmony is something a lot of current bands lack, but Ghost has it. Of course the backbone instruments are the guitars and drums, it wouldn't really be metal if it wasn't, but everything else seems to have a valid presence.<br /><br />-The catchiness. I could not get most of the songs out of my head for months. The one song in particular I cannot forget is 'Elizabeth', one of the heavier tracks. When listening to a song like 'Con Clavi Con Dio', I imagined myself in a satanic ceremony every time I sang along.<br /><br />-The use of organ in metal. Ghost has to be one of the very few metal bands that incorporate organs heavily, if not the first. And it works brilliantly.<br /><br />-The tone of some songs and vocals has a giddy, almost happy, and ceremonial vibe, yet the lyrics are lurid. Quite a unique blend, but hell, it works.<br /><br />-Expected growling vocals? Nope, they are sung cleanly for the majority of the album. The range is limited, nothing crazy like King Diamond, but it fits well here. I would've liked the vocals to be more rough/powerful (yet still clean). But alas, the clean \\"pleasant-ish\\" vocals attest to the songs being so easy for anyone to sing along to, which added to them being stuck in my head for months; as well as adding a pop sensibility. If I tried to sing along to Mercyful Fate (which I freaking love) songs, I would rupture a lung, but here, since the range is continuous, I have no problem. The vocal style also has quite a resemblance to Voivod's early 90s' style.<br /><br />-Speaking of Mercyful Fate, and also Blue Oyster Cult, here the dark melodies of the guitars, drums and bass are for a great portion of the songs a great likening to their styles.<br /><br />-Another thing about the lyrics is that they are so incessantly satanic that it's almost humorous to the person hearing them for the first time. When combined with the instruments it creates a volatile mixture that cannot be explained, but I think makes the atmosphere more \\"fiendishly-evil\\".<br /><br />-The riff from the song \\"Ritual\\" is unfortunately quite similar to Megadeth's \\"Symphony of Destruction\\", yes, but the song itself as a whole is quite different and still wonderful.<br /><br />-Some songs have a \\"scooby-doo\\" (if you've ever seen the show) sound to them, most notably the song \\"Stand by Him\\", among a few others. But instead of it sounding completely silly, it added a retro 70's doomy vibe with the top-notch musicianship.<br /><br />-If Castlevania had a soundtrack, I think this would be it. The dark mood fits that game brilliantly. When I listen to songs like \\"Elizabeth\\", \\"Death Knell\\", and the instrumental \\"Genesis\\", I sometimes think they are the backdrop of a castle in which vampires and demonic creatures are being hunted by a guy with a whip.<br /><br />-To the people who are offended by the lyrics, they are not meant to be taken seriously. I mean, this band consists of dudes who disguise themselves in black robes and the front-man is a 'demonic pope'. Do you think they'd want you to take them seriously? No, no they wouldn't, it's just all for show and presence which only adds to the great quality of the songs. All that really matters here is the music...and it rocks.<br /><br />To conclude, this collection is truly a masterpiece of catchy, doomy, poppy, dark, evil, humorous metal. I would've liked the theme to be a bit more versatile than just 'Satan, Satan, Satan 24/7' and also I would've liked the singer's voice to be a little more powerful (to be more suitable for the thrashy songs).
